Conditions of Valid Agency
Conditions for principal and agent in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions required for a valid agency contract and determine whether a given person can serve as principal or agent according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A valid agency requires the principal to have legal capacity to act and the agent to understand and intend the contract.

Agency (wakalah) is a key contract in Hanafi muamalat. Its validity hinges on the capacity of both parties. This passage from al-Quduri outlines the fundamental conditions and addresses special cases involving minors and slaves under interdiction. Understanding these conditions ensures that the contract is sound and liability is correctly assigned.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
From the first sentence, extract the condition required for the principal and the condition required for the agent.
Principal: must have authority to act and be bound by rulings. Agent: must understand and intend the contract.
2
Classify the special cases
Analyze the sentence about a minor or slave under interdiction. What additional characteristic must such an agent possess?
The minor or slave must understand buying and selling (i.e., be discerning).
3
Determine the legal consequence
When a minor or slave acts as agent, to whom do the rights and liabilities attach?
They attach to the principal, not the agent.
4
Locate the supporting proof
Find the hadith cited and identify the three categories whose actions are not binding. How does this relate to the agency case?
The hadith about the pen being lifted reinforces that minors and insane persons are not personally liable.
5
Summarize the Hanafi ruling
Combine the conditions and the special cases into a concise statement of the Hanafi position on valid agency.
Agency is valid if principal has capacity and agent understands/intends; minors/slaves can be agents but liability is on the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ شَرْطِ الْوَكَالَةِ أَنْ يَكُون الْمُوَكَّلُ مِمَّنْ يَمْلِكُ التَّصَرُّفَ وَيَلْزَمُهُ الْأَحْكَامُ وَالْوَكِيلُ مِمَّنْ يَعْقِلُ الْعَقْدَ وَيَقْصِدُهُ وَإِذَا وَكَلَ الخَرُ الْبَالِغُ أَوْ الْمَأْذُونُ لَهُ مِثْلَهُمَا جَازَ وَإِنْ وَكَلَا صَبِيًّا مَحْجُورًا عَلَيْهِ يَعْقِلُ الْبَيْعَ وَالشَّرَاءَ أَوْ عَبْدًا مَحْجُورًا عَلَيْهِ جَازَ وَلَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِمَا الْحَقُوقُ وَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِمُوَكَّلَيْهِمَا عَنْ عَلِيَّ، أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ : " رُفِعَ القَلَمُ عَنْ ثَلَاثَةٍ : عَنِ النَّائِمِ حَتَّى يَسْتَيْقِظَ ، وَعَنِ الصَّبِيِّ حَتَّى يَشِبَّ، وَعَنِ الْمَعْتُوهِ حَتَّى يَعْقِلَ "
Among the conditions of agency is that the principal be among those who own the authority to act and are bound by rulings, and the agent be among those who understand the contract and intend it. If a free adult or a permitted person appoints an agent like themselves, it is valid. And if they appoint a minor under interdiction who understands buying and selling, or a slave under interdiction, it is valid, but rights are not attached to them; they are attached to their principals. From Ali, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'The pen is lifted from three: the sleeper until he wakes, the child until he matures, and the insane until he recovers.'
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage states the essential conditions for a valid agency contract. The principal must have the legal capacity to perform the act himself and be subject to the consequences (e.g., a free adult, a minor with permission). The agent must have intellectual capacity to understand and intend the contract (عاقل). While a minor or slave under interdiction may act as an agent if they understand the transaction, the legal rights and obligations resulting from the contract do not attach to them personally but to their principals (the one who appointed them). This is because the agent lacks full legal capacity to bear rights and liabilities. The hadith about the pen being lifted from three categories (sleeper, child, insane) supports this: those without full capacity are not held accountable, so their agency is valid but they are not personally liable.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakalah): A contract by which one person appoints another to act on their behalf in a specific matter.
محجور عليه (mahjur 'alayh): A person under legal interdiction due to minority, insanity, or slavery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must the agent fulfill for a valid agency according to this passage?
A) Must be an adult.
B) Must understand and intend the contract.
C) Must be free.
D) Must be male.

Key Takeaway
For a valid agency, the principal must have legal capacity to perform the act, and the agent must have the capacity to understand and intend the contract; when a minor or slave under interdiction acts as agent, rights and liabilities attach to the principal.

Two Types of Contracts by Agents
Classification of contracts by agents and consequences for rights and liabilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of agency contracts in the Hanafi school and explain how the attribution of the contract determines liability, comparing the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
The agent's liability depends on whether he attributes the contract to himself or to his principal.

This passage clarifies a foundational principle in Hanafi agency law: the rights and obligations of a contract flow to the person to whom the contract is attributed, not necessarily the beneficial owner. Understanding this distinction is essential for anyone dealing with commercial or family transactions through agents, as it determines who can be sued and who bears responsibility. The text also uses hadith to support the Hanafi position and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the first part of the passage and note the two categories: contracts attributed to the agent himself (e.g., sale, lease) and contracts attributed to the principal (e.g., marriage, khul').
The key difference is whether the agent says 'I sell' or 'I sell on behalf of X'.
2
Understand rights and obligations
For each category, list what rights/obligations attach to whom. For sale: agent delivers, receives price, litigates defects. For marriage: principal is responsible for dower, delivery of wife.
The passage explicitly states: rights attach to the agent in the first type and to the principal in the second.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The author cites hadith: the cupping hadith (agent paid directly) and the sacrifice purchase (agent bought and sold in his own name) to show that the agent acts as a principal.
These hadith demonstrate that the agent is the direct contractor and hence liable.
4
Analyse the Shafi'i objection
Shafi'i argues that ownership belongs to the principal, so rights should attach to the principal. The Hanafi response is that the agent is the one who actually deals and has standing in court.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is who can be sued for the price.
5
Synthesise the ruling
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi: always look at the attribution. If the agent does not specify, the contract is attributed to himself by default.
This default rule is important for fatwa and daily transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْعُقُودُ الَّتِي يَعْقِدُهَا الْوَكَلَاءُ عَلَى ضَرْبَيْنِ كُلُّ عَقْدِ يُضِيفُهُ الْوَكِيلُ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ مِثْلُ الْبَيْعِ وَالْإِجَارَةِ فَحُقُوقُ ذَلِكَ الْعَقْدِ تَتَعَلَّقُ بِالْوَكِيلِ دُونَ الْمُوَكَّلِ فَيُسَلِّمُ الْمَبِيعَ وَيَقْبِضُ الثَّمَنَ وَيُطَالِبُ بِالثَّمَن إِذَا اشْتَرَى وَيَقْبِضُ الْمَبِيعَ وَيُخَاصِمُ فِي الْعَيْبِ وَكُلُّ عَقْدٍ يُضِيفُهُ إِلَى مُوَكِّلِهِ كَالنِّكَاحِ وَالخُلْعِ وَالصُّلْحِ عَنْ دَمِ الْعَمْدِ فَإِنَّ حُقُوقَهُ تَتَعَلَّقُ بِالْمُوَكَّلِ دُونَ الْوَكِيلِ فَلَا يُطَالِبُ وَكِيلُ الزَّوْجِ بِالصَّدَاقِ وَلَا يَلْزَمُ وَكِيلَ الْمَرْأَةِ تَسْلِيمُهَا وَإِذَا طَالَبَ الْمُوَكَّلُ الْمُشْتَرِيَ بِالثَّمَنِ فَلَهُ أَنْ يَمَنَعَهُ إِيَّاهُ فَإِنْ دَفَعَهُ إِلَيْهِ جَازَوَلَمْ يَكُنْ لِلْوَكِيلِ أَنْ يُطَالِبَهُ بِهِ ثَانِيًا عَنْ أَنَسِ بْن مَالِكِ قَالَ: «حَجَمَ أَبُو طَيْبَةَ رَسُولَ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَأَمَرَ لَهُ بِصَاعٍ مِنْ تَقْرٍ، وَأَمَرَ أَهْلَهُ أَنْ يُخَفِّفُوا مِنْ خَوَاجِهِ.» سَمِعْتُ سَهْلَ بْنَ سَعْدٍ السَّاعِدِيَّ يَقُولُ: «إِنِّي لَفِي الْقَوْمِ عِنْدَ رَسُولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم إِذْ قَامَتِ امْرَأَةٌ فَقَالَتْ: يَا رَسُولَ اللهِ....... فَقَالَ : هَلْ مَعَكَ مِنَ الْقُرْآنِ شَيْءٍ؟ قَالَ: مَعِي سُورَةُ كَذَا وَسُورَةُ كَذَا، قَالَ: اذْهَبْ فَقَدْ أَنْكَحْتُكَهَا بِمَا مَعَكَ مِنَ الْقُرْآنِ.» قَالَ: أَعْطَاهُ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم دِينَارًا يَشْتَرِي بِهِ أُضْحِيَّةً، أَوْ شَاةً فَاشْتَرَى شَاتَيْنِ فَبَاعَ إِحْدَاهُمَا فَدَعَا لَهُ بِالْبَرَكَةِ فِي بَيْعِهِ كَانَ لَوِ اشْتَرَى تُرَابًا لَرَبِحَ فِيهِ
The contracts that agents make are of two types: every contract that the agent adds to himself, like sale and lease, the rights of that contract attach to the agent, not the principal. So he delivers the sold item, receives the price, demands the price if he bought, receives the purchased item, and litigates regarding defects. And every contract that he adds to his principal, like marriage, khul', and reconciliation for intentional killing, its rights attach to the principal, not the agent. So the agent of the husband does not demand the dower, nor is the agent of the woman obligated to hand her over. And if the principal demands the price from the buyer, he may prevent him from paying the agent; if he pays him, it is valid, and the agent may not demand it again. From Anas: 'Abu Taybah cupped the Messenger of Allah, so he ordered for him a sa' of dates...' I heard Sahl ibn Sa'd as-Sa'idi: '...he said: Go, I have married her to you for what you have of the Quran.' He said: 'The Prophet gave him a dinar to buy a sacrifice, so he bought two sheep and sold one...'
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage classifies agency contracts into two types based on whom the agent attributes the contract to. In the first type, the agent attributes the contract to himself (e.g., in sale or lease), so all rights and obligations (delivery, payment, litigation for defects) attach to the agent, not the principal. In the second type, the agent attributes the contract directly to the principal (e.g., marriage, khul', reconciliation in homicide), so rights attach to the principal; the agent cannot demand dower or be responsible for delivery. The text also provides hadith as evidence: the cupping hadith shows the Prophet (saw) paid the agent, implying agent handles payment; the marriage hadith shows the agent (the woman's son) performed the marriage adding it to the principal; the story of buying a sacrifice shows an agent buying and selling—the Prophet gave the agent a dinar, and the agent acted in his own name. The commentary notes an usul principle: Hanafi: the agent is the real and legal contractor, so rights attach to him. Shafi'i: ownership is for the principal, so rights attach to the principal.

Key Terms
إضافة العقد إلى نفسه (idafat al-'aqd ila nafsih): The agent performs the contract in his own name, as if he is the principal.
إضافة العقد إلى موكله (idafat al-'aqd ila muwakkilih): The agent performs the contract expressly on behalf of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent buys a car in his own name for his friend (principal), who is responsible to pay the seller according to the Hanafi school?
A) The principal
B) The agent
C) Both equally
D) Neither; the contract is invalid

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines liability in agency contracts by examining to whom the agent attributes the contract.

Specification in Agency to Purchase
Ruling on requirement to specify item when appointing agent to purchase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for appointing an agent to purchase in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish between a restricted and general agency.

Core Idea
A purchasing agent must know what to buy—specify type and description or type and price, unless authority is general.

When you authorize someone to buy for you, you must clearly define what they can purchase. The Hanafi school balances flexibility and clarity: general agency grants broad discretion, but a restricted agency requires specification to avoid disputes. This lesson examines the rule and its exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
Recognize that the ruling concerns the appointment of an agent (wakalah) specifically to purchase an item for the principal.
2
State the Default Rule
Understand that in a restricted agency, the principal must specify either the type (jins) and description (sifah) of the item, or the type and the price (thaman). This is necessary to prevent ambiguity (gharar).
‘Necessary’ (fala budda) indicates a condition for validity.
3
Recognize the Exception
If the principal uses a general agency (wakalah ‘ammah), such as saying ‘Buy for me what you see,’ then no specification is needed. The agent has discretionary authority.
General agency is valid and gives the agent broad discretion.
4
Apply the Distinction
Given a statement from a principal, classify it as restricted or general and determine whether the agency is valid. For restricted, verify that type+description or type+price is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ وَكَّلَ رَجُلًا لِيَشْتَرِيَ لَهُ شَيْئًا فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَسْمِيَةِ جِنْسِهِ وَصِفَتِهِ أَوْجِنْسِهِ وَمَبْلَعْ ثَنِهِ إِلَّا أَنْ يُوَكَّلَهُ وَكَالَةً عَامَّةً فَيَقُولَ لَهُ ابْتَعْ لِي مَا رَأَيْت
And whoever appoints a man to buy something for him, it is necessary to specify its type and description, or its type and amount of its price, unless he appoints him with a general agency by saying to him: 'Buy for me what you see.'
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This is a ruling on the specification required in an agency to purchase. The principal must specify either the type (jins) and description (sifa) of the item, or the type and price (thaman). If the agency is general, such as saying 'Buy for me what you see,' then no further specification is needed. This prevents ambiguity and potential disputes. The hadith about Urwa al-Bariqi (given a dinar to buy a sacrifice) is cited as a proof: the Prophet gave him a specific price and type, not a general agency. Another hadith from Jabir supports the validity of general agency. The Hanafi school differentiates: in a restricted agency, specification is required; in a general agency, the agent has discretion.

Key Terms
وكالة عامة (wakalah 'ammah): A general agency where the principal gives broad authority without specifying items.
تسمية الجنس والصفة (tasmiyat al-jins wa al-sifah): Specifying the category and characteristics of the item to be purchased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The color only
B) The type and description, or type and price
C) Just the price
D) No specification is needed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a purchasing agency requires specification of type and description or type and price, unless the agency is general (e.g., 'buy what you see').

Agent's Acknowledgement in Court: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on agent's acknowledgement in court with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on agent's acknowledgement in court and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad from Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgement in court binds the agent, not the principal, per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

When an agent appears in court, his statements may affect his own liability, but do they bind the person who appointed him? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this point. This lesson unpacks the rule and its practical implications for litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the agent's acknowledgement (iqrar) made during court proceedings. The ruling: the acknowledgement is valid (ja'iz) for the agent himself, but according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it does not bind the principal in court.
The acknowledgement is not invalid; it simply does not transfer liability to the principal.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf disagrees: he holds that the agent's acknowledgement is valid against the principal outside the court, though not inside the court. This is a partial concession.
The dispute is about the scope of the agent's authority to bind the principal.
3
Analyse the basis of each view
The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) stems from the principle that an agent's authority is limited to acts that are explicitly or implicitly within his power. Acknowledging liability in court is not within the default authority of an agent. Abu Yusuf's view may rely on the broader concept of representation, but he still confines it outside the formal court setting.
Both views agree that inside court, the agent cannot bind the principal.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The agent's acknowledgement in court does not bind the principal at all.
This is the view adopted in Zahir al-Riwayah.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Suppose an agent is sued and he admits the debt in court. The principal can deny it and is not liable. However, if the acknowledgement occurs outside court, Abu Yusuf would hold the principal bound, but the majority would not.
The practical effect is that the principal must be cautious about whom he appoints as agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا أَقَرَّ الْوَكِيلُ بِالْخُصُومَةِ عِنْدَ الْقَاضِي جَازَ إِقْرَارُهُ وَلَا يَجُوزُ إِقْرَارُهُ عَلَيْهِ عِنْدَ الْقَاضِي عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ يَجُوزُ إِقْرَارُهُ عَلَيْهِ عِنْدَ غَيْرِ الْقَاضِي ٢٥ وَمَنْ ادَّعَى أَنَّهُ وَكِيلُ الْغَائِبِ فِي قَبْضِ دَيْنِهِ فَصَدَّقَهُ الْغَرِيمُ أُمِرَ بِتَسْلِيمِ الدَّيْنِ إِلَيْهِ فَإِنْ حَضَرَ الْغَائِبُ فَصَدَّقَهُ وَإِلَّا دَفَعَ الْغَرِيمُ إِلَيْهِ الدَّيْنَ ثَانِيًا وَيَرْجِعُ بِهِ عَلَى الْوَكِيلِ إِنْ كَانَ بَاقِيًا فِي يَدِهِ وَإِنْ قَالَ إِنِّي وَكِيلُ الْغَائِبِ بِقَبْضِ الْوَدِيعَةِ وَصَدَّقَهُ الْمُودَعُ ثُمَّ لَمْ يُؤْمَرُ بِالتَّسْلِيمِ إِلَيْهِ
وجه : (١) الحديث لثبوت وَإِذَا أَقَرَّ الْوَكِيلُ بِالْخُصُومَةِ عِنْدَ الْقَاضِي جَازَ إِقْرَارُهُ \ وَزَعَمَ عُرْوَةُ: أَنَّ مَرْوَانَ بْنَ الْحَكَمِ وَالْمِسْوَرَ بْنَ مَحْرَمَةَ أَخْبَرَاهُ: «أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَامَ حِينَ جَاءَهُ وَفْدُ هَوَازِنَ فَقَالَ النَّاسُ قَدْ طَيَّبْنَا ذَلِكَ لِرَسُولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم لَهُمْ ، فَقَالَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: إنَّا لَا نَدْرِي مَنْ أَذِنَ مِنْكُمْ فِي ذَلِكَ مِمَّنْ لَمْ يَأْذَنْ، فَارْجِعُوا حَتَّى يَرْفَعُوا إِلَيْنَا عُرَفَاؤُكُمْ أَمْرَكُمْ فَرَجَعَ النَّاسُ ، فَكَلَّمَهُمْ عُرَفَاؤُهُمْ، ثُمَّ رَجَعُوا إِلَى رَسُولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَأَخْبَرُوهُ: أَنَّهُمْ قَدْ طَيَّبُوا مُسْلِمِينَ. وَأَذِنُوا.»، (بخاري شريف، بَابٌ : إِذَا وَهَبَ شَيْئًا لِوَكِيل أَوْ شَفِيعٍ قَوْمٍ، نمبر 2307)
وجه : (٢) الحديث لثبوت وَمَنْ وَكَّلَ رَجُلًا لِيَشْتَرِي لَهُ شَيْئًا عَن ابْنِ عُمَرَ، عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم عَمَعْنَاهُ، قَالَ: «وَمَنْ أَعَانَ عَلَى خُصُومَةٍ بِظُلْمٍ فَقَدْ بَاءَ بِغَضَبٍ مِنَ اللَّهِ عز وجل»،(سنن ابوداود شریف، بَابٌ فِيمَنْ يُعِينُ عَلَى خُصُومَةٍ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يَعْلَمَ أَمْرَهَا، نمبر 3598)
٢٤ اصول: عین چیز واپس لینے میں وکیل مقدمہ کا خصم نہیں ہوتا اسلئے قابض نے مقدمہ دائر کر ایاتو وکیل کا قبضہ تو نہیں کرے گا، لیکن بیچ بھی ثابت نہیں ہو گی۔

If the agent acknowledges in litigation before the judge, his acknowledgment is valid, but his acknowledgment against [the principal] is not valid before the judge according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: His acknowledgment against him is valid before other than the judge. 25 And whoever claims that he is the agent of an absent person for collecting his debt, and the debtor confirms him, he is ordered to hand over the debt to him. If the absent person comes and confirms him, then [it is fine]; otherwise, the debtor pays the debt a second time to him, and he recovers it from the agent if it remains in his hand. If he says: I am the agent of the absent person for collecting the deposit, and the depositee confirms him, then he is not ordered to hand over to him.

Point: (2) The hadith for establishing: ...
24 Principle: In taking back the specific item, the agent is not an opponent in litigation...
25 Principle: One can appoint an agent over one's own property and hand it over to him.
Principle: The property of another cannot be handed over despite confirmation of agency.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Wakalah presents two rulings with intra-Hanafi disagreement. First, regarding the agent's acknowledgement in court: if the agent acknowledges something during litigation, that acknowledgement is valid as far as his own liability, but according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, it cannot bind the principal (the one who appointed him) in court. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, holding that the agent's acknowledgement is valid against the principal outside court, but not in court. The second ruling deals with a person who claims to be the agent of an absent person for collecting a debt. If the debtor confirms this claim, the judge orders the debtor to pay the debt to the claimant. Later, if the absent principal appears and confirms the agency, no further payment is needed; if he denies it, the debtor must pay again to the principal and can recover from the false agent if the money is still with him. However, if the claimed agency is for collecting a deposit rather than a debt, the depositee is not ordered to hand over the deposit to the claimant, because deposits are trusts and cannot be transferred without certainty of ownership. The hadith proofs and usul principles reinforce these distinctions.

Key Terms
وَكِيل (wakīl): Agent appointed to perform a specific legal act.
إِقْرَار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession, legally binding on the one who makes it.
خُصُومَة (khuṣūmah): Litigation; a legal dispute brought before a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The principal is not bound by the acknowledgement in court.
D) The acknowledgement is invalid entirely.

Key Takeaway
An agent's court acknowledgement binds the agent, not the principal, in the majority Hanafi view.

Claim of Agency for an Absent Person: Debt vs Deposit
Ruling on claim of agency for absent debtor vs deposit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rule for a claimant of agency for an absent debtor versus a depositor, and apply the consequences of false claim.

Core Idea
A claimant for an absent debtor's debt is paid upon debtor's confirmation; for a deposit, no delivery occurs despite confirmation.

When someone claims to be the agent of an absent person, the ruling differs based on whether the claim is for collecting a debt or a deposit. This lesson examines the reason for the distinction and the practical outcome if the claim turns out false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario A: Claimant says 'I am agent of absent person to collect his debt'. Scenario B: Claimant says 'I am agent to collect his deposit.' The debtor/depositee confirms the claim.
The debt is a liability; the deposit is a trust.
2
State the ruling for debt
If the debtor confirms the claim, the judge orders the debtor to pay the debt to the claimant. If the absent person returns and confirms, all good; otherwise, the debtor must pay again to the principal and can recover from the agent.
The debtor bears the risk of paying a false agent initially.
3
State the ruling for deposit
If the depositee confirms the claim, the judge does NOT order the deposit to be handed over. The depositee remains liable to return the deposit to the owner.
Deposit is a trust; it cannot be transferred based on mere claim of agency.
4
Analyse the rationale
Debt can be discharged by payment to any authorized person; deposit is a specific trust that requires certainty of ownership. The principal's property is not at risk in debt case because the debtor can recover from the false agent.
The distinction protects the owner's property in deposit cases.
5
Apply the consequences
If the claim is false: for debt, the debtor pays twice but recovers from the agent; for deposit, no payment is made, so the depositee is protected.
The false agent is liable for any loss caused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اصول: غیر کی چیز وکالت کی تصدیق کے باوجو د حوالہ نہیں کیا جاسکتا ہے۔

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Wakalah presents two rulings with intra-Hanafi disagreement. First, regarding the agent's acknowledgement in court: if the agent acknowledges something during litigation, that acknowledgement is valid as far as his own liability, but according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, it cannot bind the principal (the one who appointed him) in court. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, holding that the agent's acknowledgement is valid against the principal outside court, but not in court. The second ruling deals with a person who claims to be the agent of an absent person for collecting a debt. If the debtor confirms this claim, the judge orders the debtor to pay the debt to the claimant. Later, if the absent principal appears and confirms the agency, no further payment is needed; if he denies it, the debtor must pay again to the principal and can recover from the false agent if the money is still with him. However, if the claimed agency is for collecting a deposit rather than a debt, the depositee is not ordered to hand over the deposit to the claimant, because deposits are trusts and cannot be transferred without certainty of ownership. The hadith proofs and usul principles reinforce these distinctions.

Key Terms
غَرِيم (gharīm): Debtor; the person who owes the debt.
وَدِيعَة (wadī'ah): Deposit; an item entrusted to someone for safekeeping.
مُودَع (mūda'): Depositee; the person who holds the deposit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a depositee confirms a claimant's agency, what happens?
A) The depositee must hand over the deposit to the claimant.
B) The depositee is not ordered to hand over the deposit.
C) The depositee hands over only if the claimant provides a written authorization.
D) The depositee must ask the absent owner for permission.

Key Takeaway
A claim of agency for debt leads to payment upon confirmation; for deposit, it does not result in delivery.

Hawalah and the Tawa Dispute
States permissibility of hawalah, conditions, effect, and intra-Hanafi dispute on tawa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hawalah, list its conditions, and distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's definitions of tawa.

Core Idea
Hawalah discharges the original debtor; the creditor's right to revert is limited to tawa.

This lesson covers the basic ruling of hawalah (transfer of debt) as presented in Al-Quduri's matn. You will learn that hawalah is permissible, requires three-party consent, and fully discharges the transferor. The key nuance is the definition of 'tawa'—the loss that allows the creditor to revert to the original debtor. Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions disagreed on the scenarios constituting tawa, and this lesson unpacks that intra-Hanafi dispute with its evidentiary proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and its ruling
Recognize that hawalah is a transfer of debt from the transferor to the transferee, and it is permissible (ja'iz) by the Sunnah.
The ruling is mubah, not obligatory.
2
List the conditions for validity
Note that the contract requires the consent of all three parties: transferor (muhil), creditor (muhtal), and transferee (muhal alayh).
3
State the effect on the transferor
Upon completion, the transferor is fully discharged from the debt; the creditor cannot revert to him unless his right is 'tawa' (lost).
This is a key distinction from kafalah, where the original debtor remains liable.
4
Define tawa according to Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa restricts tawa to two cases: (1) the transferee denies the hawalah and swears an oath with no evidence, or (2) the transferee dies insolvent.
This is the primary position in the matn.
5
Add the Sahibayn's third case
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad add a third scenario: the judge declares the transferee insolvent during his lifetime.
This is a secondary position, but some later fatwa works adopt it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الْحَوَالَةِ
قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ - الحَوَالَةُ جَائِزَةُ بِالدُّيُونِ وَيَصِحُ بِرِضًا الْمُحِيلِ وَالْمُحْتَالِ وَالْمُحَالِ عَلَيْهِ ا فَإِذَا تَمَّتْ الْحَوَالَةُ بَرِئَ الْمُحِيلُ مِنْ الدَّيْنِ وَلَمْ يَرْجِعْ الْمُحْتَالُ عَلَى الْمُحِيلِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَتْوَى حَقَّهُ وَالْتَوَى عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ بِأَحَدِ أَمْرَيْنِ إِمَّا أَنْ يَجْحَدَ الْحَوَالَةَ وَيَحْلِفَ وَلَا بَيْنَةَ عَلَيْهِ أَوْ يَمُوتَ مُفْلِسًا وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ وَجْهَا ثَالِثَا وَهُوَ أَنْ يَحْكُمَ الْحَاكِمُ بِفَلَسِهِ فِي حَالِ حَيَاتِهِ
لوجه: (1) الحديث لثبوت الْحَوَالَةُ جَائِزَةٌ بِالدُّيُونِ \ عَنْ ابْنِ عُمَرَ، عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «مَطْلُ الغَنِي ظُلْمٌ، وَإِذَا أُحِلْتَ عَلَى مَلِيءٍ فَاتَّبَعْهُ، وَلَا تَبِعْ بَيْعَتَيْنِ فِي بَيْعَةٍ»(سنن الترمذي، بَابُ مَا جَاءَ في مطل الغني أَنَّهُ ظُلْمٌ، نمبر 1309/ سنن ابن ماجه ، بَابُ الحَوَالَةِ، نمبر 1309/)
وجه : (۲) الحديث لثبوت الحَوَالَةُ جَائِزَةٌ بِالدُّيُونِ \ قَالَ أَبُو قَتَادَةَ: صَلِّ عَلَيْهِ يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ وَعَلَيَّ دَيْنُهُ، فَصَلَّى عَلَيْهِ.» (بخاري شريف بَابٌ : إِنْ أَحَالَ دَيْنَ الْمَيِّتِ عَلَى رَجُلٍ جَازَ، نمبر (2289) وجه : (۱) قول الصحابي لثبوت فَإِذَا تَمَّتْ الْحَوَالَةُ بَرِئَ الْمُحِيلُ مِنْ الدَّيْنِ عَنْ عُثْمَانَ بْن عَفَّانَ قَالَ: لَيْسَ عَلَى مَالِ امْرِئٍ مُسْلِمٍ تَوَى يَعْنِي حَوَالَةً، الكبري لليبيهقي، بَابُ مَنْ قَالَ: يَرْجِعُ عَلَى الْمُحِيلِ، لَا تَوَى عَلَى مَالِ مُسْلِمٍ، 11391/ ابن شيبه، فِي الْحَوَالَةِ، أَلَهُ أَنْ يَرْجِعَ فِيهَا،20730) وجه:(۲)قول التابعي لثبوت فَإِذَا تَمَّتْ الْحَوَالَةُ بَرِئَ الْمُحِيلُ مِنْ الدَّيْنِ عَنْ شُرَيْحِ، فِي الرَّجُلِ يُحِيلُ الرَّجُلَ فَيَتْوَى قَالَ: «لَا يَرْجِعُ عَلَى الْأَوَّلِ مصنف ابن ابي شيبه، فِي الْحَوَالَةِ، أَلَهُ أَنْ يَرْجِعَ فيها، نمبر 20727)
وجه : (۱) قول التابعي لثبوت وَالْتَوَى عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ بِأَحَدِ أَمْرَيْنِ عَنِ الْحَكَمِ بْنِ عُتَيْبَةَ، قَالَ: «لَا يَرْجِعُ فِي الْحَوَالَةِ إِلَى صَاحِبِهِ حَتَّى يُفْلِسَ أَوْ يَمُوتَ وَلَا يَدَعُ، فَإِنَّ الرَّجُلَ يُوسِرُ مَرَّةً، وَيُعْسِرُ مَرَّةً» ( مصنف ابن ابي شيبه، فِي الْحَوَالَةِ، أَلَهُ أَنْ يَرْجِعَ فِيهَا، نمبر 20723)
توجه : (۱) قول التابعى لثبوت وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ وَجْهَا ثَالِثَا \ قَالَ: «لَا يَرْجِعُ فِي الْحَوَالَةِ إِلَى صَاحِبِهِ حَتَّى يُفْلِسَ» مصنف ابن ابي شيبه، فِي الْحَوَالَةِ، أَلَهُ أَنْ يَرْجِعَ فِيهَا، نمبر 20723) اصول دین اصل مقروض سے کفیل کی طرف چلا جائے اور پھر کفیل ہی ذمہ دار ہو۔ اصول: کفالہ کی طرح حوالہ بھی درست ہے کیونکہ ایسی چیز لازم کی جاتی ہے جسکو سپرد کرنے پر قدرت ہے۔
Book of Hawalah. He (the author) said - may Allah have mercy on him - Hawalah is permissible for debts and is valid with the consent of the transferor, the creditor, and the transferee. So when the hawalah is completed, the transferor is discharged from the debt, and the creditor does not have recourse against the transferor unless his right is lost. And loss, according to Abu Hanifah, is one of two things: either that (the transferee) denies the hawalah and takes an oath while there is no evidence against him, or he dies insolvent. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said there is a third way: that the judge declares him insolvent during his lifetime. Evidence: (1) The hadith for the establishment that hawalah is permissible for debts: From Ibn Umar, from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'The procrastination of the wealthy is oppression, and when you are referred to a solvent person, follow him, and do not combine two sales in one.' (2) The hadith for the establishment that hawalah is permissible for debts: Abu Qatadah said: 'Pray for him, O Messenger of Allah, and his debt is upon me,' so he prayed for him. Evidence: (1) The saying of the Companion for the establishment that when hawalah is completed, the transferor is discharged from the debt: From Uthman ibn Affan who said: 'There is no loss on the property of a Muslim,' meaning hawalah. (2) The saying of the Tabi'i for the establishment that when hawalah is completed, the transferor is discharged from the debt: From Shurayh, about a man who makes a hawalah and then his right is lost, he said: 'He does not have recourse against the first.' Evidence: (1) The saying of the Tabi'i for the establishment that loss according to Abu Hanifah is one of two things: From al-Hakam ibn Utaybah, who said: 'He does not have recourse in hawalah to his companion until he becomes insolvent or dies and leaves nothing, for a man is wealthy at times and poor at times.' Evidence: (1) The saying of the Tabi'i for the establishment that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said there is a third way: He said: 'He does not have recourse in hawalah to his companion until he becomes insolvent.' Principle: The debt moves from the original debtor to the guarantor, and then the guarantor becomes responsible. Principle: Just like kafalah, hawalah is valid because it is an obligation that one has the ability to fulfill.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is from the matn of Al-Quduri on the Book of Hawalah, followed by evidentiary proofs from hadith, sayings of Companions, and Tabi'in. The ruling states that Hawalah (transfer of debt) is permissible for debts and becomes valid only with the consent of all three parties: the transferor (original debtor), the creditor, and the transferee (new debtor). Upon completion of the transfer, the transferor is completely discharged from the debt, and the creditor cannot revert to him unless his right is 'tawa' (lost). The author then presents the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the definition of 'tawa' (loss). Imam Abu Hanifa restricts it to two scenarios: (1) the transferee denies the hawalah and swears an oath in the absence of proof, or (2) the transferee dies insolvent. Imams Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) add a third scenario: (3) a judge declares the transferee insolvent during his lifetime. The provided proofs include the hadith prohibiting procrastination of the wealthy and commanding to follow a solvent person, the practice of the Prophet praying for someone with debt, and the statements of Uthman, Shurayh, and al-Hakam ibn Utaybah supporting Abu Hanifa's positions. The usul commentary at the end explains that the principle of hawalah is analogous to kafalah (suretyship) in that the liability shifts to the new obligor.

Key Terms
الحَوَالَةُ (al-hawalah): Transfer of a debt from one debtor (transferor) to another (transferee), discharging the original debtor.
التَوَى (at-tawa): Loss of the creditor's right, allowing him to revert to the original debtor. Defined differently by Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
الْمُحِيلُ (al-muhil): The transferor, i.e., the original debtor who transfers the debt.
الْمُحْتَالُ (al-muhtal): The creditor who accepts the transfer and becomes entitled to claim from the transferee.
الْمُحَالُ عَلَيْهِ (al-muhal alayh): The transferee, i.e., the person to whom the debt is transferred and who becomes liable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, which of the following constitutes 'tawa' (loss) allowing the creditor to revert to the transferor?
A) The transferee denies the hawalah and takes an oath with no evidence.
B) The transferee dies insolvent.
C) A judge declares the transferee insolvent during his lifetime.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
In hawalah, the transferor is discharged unless tawa occurs; the definition of tawa is disputed within the Hanafi school, with Abu Hanifa limiting it to denial with oath or death-insolvency.

6 min
Disputes in Transfer of Debt (Hawalah): Two Rulings
Two rulings on conflicting claims in hawalah transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two evidentiary rulings in hawalah disputes and apply them to determine who bears the burden of proof.

Core Idea
In hawalah disputes, the assignor is believed only when the assignee sues, not when the drawee sues.

This lesson examines two specific scenarios from the Hanafi school regarding hawalah (transfer of debt). The first scenario involves a dispute between the assignor and the drawee; the second between the assignor and the assignee. Understanding these rulings is crucial for resolving real-world disputes over debt transfers, as they establish the default presumptions in the absence of clear evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties in each scenario
The first scenario involves the drawee (muhaal 'alayh) suing the assignor (muheel). The second involves the assignor suing the assignee (muhtaal).
Keep track of who is claiming what.
2
Understand the nature of each claim
In the first, the assignor claims the hawalah was for a debt the drawee owed him. In the second, the assignor claims the hawalah was only for collection, not payment.
The assignor's claim differs in each scenario.
3
Apply the evidentiary ruling
In the first scenario, the assignor's claim is rejected outright; he must pay. In the second, the assignor's claim is accepted with his oath.
Note the difference in outcome.
4
Identify the underlying principle
The default presumption favors the assignor as the original debtor, but this is overridden when the drawee sues because the drawee's claim is based on the debt he paid to the assignor.
The principle prevents the assignor from reneging on the transfer.
5
Recognize the practical outcome
In practice, the assignor cannot avoid paying the drawee by claiming the transfer was for a debt; but he can avoid paying the assignee by claiming agency, subject to oath.
This protects the drawee's expectancy of repayment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا طَالَبَ الْمُحَالُ عَلَيْهِ الْمُحِيلَ بِمِثْلِ مَالِ الْحَوَالَةِ، فَقَالَ الْمُحِيلُ أَحَلْت بِدَيْنٍ لِي عَلَيْكَ لَمْ يُقْبَلْ قَوْلُهُ وَكَانَ عَلَيْهِ مِثْلُ الدَّيْنِ. وَإِنْ طَالَبَ الْمُحِيلُ الْمُحْتَالَ بِمَا أَحَالَهُ بِهِ، وَقَالَ إِنَّمَا أَحَلْتُكَ لِتَقْبِضَهُ لِي، وَقَالَ الْمُحْتَالُ أَحَلْتِنِي بِدَيْنٍ لِي عَلَيْكَ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْمُحِيلِ مَعَ يَمِينِهِ
When the drawee (muhaal 'alayh) demands from the assignor (muheel) the like of the hawalah debt, and the assignor says, 'I transferred a debt that I had against you,' his statement is not accepted, and he must pay the like of the debt. And when the assignor demands from the assignee (muhtaal) what he transferred to him, and says, 'I only transferred to you for you to collect it for me,' and the assignee says, 'You transferred to me a debt that you owed me,' then the statement of the assignor is accepted with his oath.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from the Hanafi school regarding disputes in hawalah (transfer of debt). The first ruling: if the drawee (the person to whom the debt is transferred, who owes money to the assignor) is sued by the assignor for repayment, and the assignor claims that the hawalah was for a debt he owed to the assignee (i.e., he transferred a debt that the drawee owed to him), this claim is not accepted. Instead, the assignor remains liable to the drawee for the amount. The second ruling: if the assignor sues the assignee (the creditor who received the transfer) for the amount transferred, claiming that the transfer was only for collection (i.e., the assignee was to collect from the drawee and then pay the assignor), and the assignee claims it was a payment of a debt owed to him, the assignor's statement is accepted with his oath. This is because the assignor is the original debtor and his claim is supported by the default presumption that the debt is still owed to him. These rulings are based on the principle that in ambiguous situations, the original debtor is believed unless proven otherwise.

Key Terms
حَوَالَة (hawalah): Transfer of debt from one party to another
مُحِيل (muheel): The assignor or transferor of the debt
مُحْتَال (muhtaal): The assignee or person to whom the debt is transferred
مَحَال عَلَيْهِ (muhaal 'alayh): The drawee or person upon whom the debt is transferred

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first dispute (drawee suing assignor), what happens when the assignor claims he transferred a debt owed by the drawee?
A) His claim is accepted with oath
B) His claim is not accepted and he must pay
C) The case is dismissed
D) The assignee is sued instead

Key Takeaway
In hawalah disputes, the assignor is believed against the assignee but not against the drawee.

The Three Types of Sulh and Their Rulings
Categorizing sulh into three types with proofs from Quran and Sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of sulh based on the defendant's stance and state the legal consequences of each, including the application of shuf'a and recovery of compensation.

Core Idea
Sulh is valid in all three forms—acknowledgment, silence, or denial—provided it does not legalize the unlawful.

This lesson examines a foundational passage from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar on the contract of sulh (settlement/compromise). The text categorizes sulh into three types based on the defendant's response to a claim: acknowledgment, silence, or denial. It then clarifies the legal nature of each type and provides specific sub-rulings on shuf'a (preemption) and the recovery of compensation when a third party reclaims property. Finally, the passage presents four proofs from the Quran and Sunnah to establish the permissibility and limits of sulh. By working through this passage, you will learn not only the classifications but also the practical implications of each category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its classification
Read the opening sentence: sulh is of three types. Note that all three are declared permissible (ja'iz). This is the core ruling.
The three types are based on the defendant's stance: iqrar (acknowledgment), sukut (silence), and inkar (denial).
2
Understand the legal nature of each type
For sulh with iqrar, the contract takes on the rules of a sale if it involves property for property, or of ijarah if property for usufruct. For sulh with silence or denial, it functions as an oath-ransoming for the defendant and as an exchange for the plaintiff.
This distinction affects the rights and obligations of the parties, e.g., whether the contract must meet the strict conditions of sale.
3
Apply the sub-rulings on shuf'a and recovery
Learn that shuf'a (preemption) applies only when sulh is used to acquire a house (sulh 'ala dar), not when relinquishing a claim (sulh 'an dar). Also, if after a sulh with iqrar a third party proves ownership of part of the property, the defendant can recover a proportional part of the compensation from the plaintiff.
These are practical outcomes that illustrate how the legal nature of sulh affects property rights.
4
Analyze the proofs (adillah)
The passage cites one verse: Qur'an 4:128 (sulh between spouses is good), and three hadith: the Hudaybiyyah treaty, the general hadith 'sulh is permissible among Muslims' (with exception of making haram halal), and the hadith urging seeking absolution before the Day of Judgment. Note the exception: sulh that changes the lawful/unlawful is invalid.
These proofs establish the original permissibility of sulh and its limits.

Source Text

قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى - الصُّلْحُ عَلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَضْرُبٍ صُلْحٌ مَعَ إِقْرَارٍ وَصُلْحٌ مَعَ سُكُوتٍ وَهُوَ أَنْ لَا يُقرَّ الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ وَلَا يُنْكِرُ وَصُلْحٌ مَعَ إِنْكَارٍ وَكُلُّ ذَلِكَ جَائِزٌ. فَإِنْ وَقَعَ الصُّلْحُ عَلَى الْإِقْرَارِ اعْتُبِرَ فِيهِ مَا يُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْبَيَاعَاتِ إِنْ وَقَعَ عَنْ مَالٍ بِمَالٍ وَإِنْ وَقَعَ عَنْ مَالِ بِمَنَافِعَ اعْتُبِرَ بِالْإِجَارَاتِ. وَالصُّلْحُ عَنْ السُّكُوتِ وَالْإِنْكَارِ فِي حَقِ الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ لِافْتِدَاءِ الْيَمِينِ وَقَطْعِ الْخُصُومَةِ وَفِي حَقِّ الْمُدَّعِي بِمَعْنَى الْمُعَاوَضَةِ. وَإِذَا صَالَحَ عَنْ دَارٍ لَمْ تَجِبْ فِيهَا الشُّفْعَةُ وَإِذَا صَالَحَ عَلَى دَارٍ وَجَبَتْ فِيهَا الشُّفْعَةُ. وَإِذَا كَانَ الصُّلْحُ عَنْ إِقْرَارٍ فَاسْتُحِقَّ بَعْضُ الْمُصَالَحِ عَنْهُ رَجَعَ الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ بِحِصَّةِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ الْعِوَضِ. وَجْهٌ (1): الْآيَةُ: ﴿وَإِنِ امْرَأَةٌ خَافَتْ مِنْ بَعْلِهَا نُشُورًا أَوْ إِعْرَاضًا فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِمَا أَن يُصْلِحَا بَيْنَهُمَا صُلْحًا وَالصُّلْحُ خَيْرٌ﴾ [النساء: 128]. وَجْهٌ (2): الْحَدِيثُ: عَنِ الْبَرَاءِ بْنِ عَازِبٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمَا قَالَ: صَالَحَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ يَوْمَ الْحُدَيْبِيَةِ عَلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَشْيَاءَ: عَلَى أَنَّ مَنْ أَتَاهُ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ رَدَّهُ إِلَيْهِمْ، وَمَنْ أَتَاهُمْ مِنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ لَمْ يَرُدُّوهُ. (الْبُخَارِيُّ، بَابُ الصُّلْحِ مَعَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ). وَجْهٌ (3): الْحَدِيثُ: عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ: «الصُّلْحُ جَائِزٌ بَيْنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ» زَادَ أَحْمَدُ: «إِلَّا صُلْحًا أَحَلَّ حَرَامًا أَوْ حَرَّمَ حَلَالًا». (سُنَنُ أَبِي دَاوُدَ، بَابٌ فِي الصُّلْحِ). وَجْهٌ (4): الْحَدِيثُ: عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ: «مَنْ كَانَتْ لَهُ مَظْلِمَةٌ لِأَحَدٍ مِنْ عِرْضِهِ أَوْ شَيْءٍ فَلْيَتَحَلَّلْهُ مِنْهُ الْيَوْمَ قَبْلَ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ دِينَارٌ وَلَا دِرْهَمٌ». (الْبُخَارِيُّ، بَابُ مَنْ كَانَتْ لَهُ مَظْلَمَةٌ عِنْدَ الرَّجُلِ فَحَلَّلَهَا لَهُ هَلْ يُبَيِّنُ مَظْلَمَتَهُ). أُصُولُ شَرِيعَتِ مَيْں ہَرْ صُلْح جَائِز ہے، سِوائے وہ جو حَرَام کو حَلَال کَر دے یا حَلَال کو حَرَام کَر دے۔
He said - may Allah have mercy on him - Sulh is of three types: sulh with acknowledgment, sulh with silence (where the defendant neither admits nor denies), and sulh with denial. All of that is permissible. If sulh occurs with acknowledgment, it is treated according to what is considered in sales if it involves property for property, and if it involves property for benefits, it is treated according to Ijarah (hire). Sulh involving silence and denial is, with respect to the defendant, for the purpose of ransoming the oath and ending the dispute, and with respect to the plaintiff, it is in the sense of an exchange. When one makes sulh (settlement) relinquishing a house, preemption (shuf'a) is not obligatory, but when one makes sulh to acquire a house, preemption becomes obligatory. If the sulh was with acknowledgment and part of the property settled is recovered by a rightful claimant, the defendant recovers from the compensation (given by the plaintiff) a proportion corresponding to that part. Evidence (1): The verse: 'And if a woman fears from her husband discord or turning away, there is no sin upon them if they make peace between them, and peace is good.' (An-Nisa 4:128). Evidence (2): The hadith from al-Bara' ibn 'Azib that the Prophet (peace be upon him) made peace with the polytheists on the day of Hudaybiyyah on three conditions. (Bukhari). Evidence (3): The hadith from Abu Hurayrah that the Prophet said: 'Sulh is permissible among Muslims,' with Ahmad's addition: 'except a sulh that makes lawful what is unlawful or unlawful what is lawful.' (Abu Dawud). Evidence (4): The hadith from Abu Hurayrah: 'Whoever has a wrong done to him regarding another's honor or anything else should obtain his release today before there is no dinar or dirham.' (Bukhari). Principles of Shari'ah: Every sulh is permissible except that which makes the unlawful lawful or the lawful unlawful.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar opens the chapter on Sulh (compromise/settlement). The author presents the ruling that sulh is of three types based on the defendant's position: (1) with acknowledgment, (2) with silence (neither admitting nor denying the claim), and (3) with denial. All three are valid in Islamic law. The commentary then explains the legal nature of each type. Sulh with acknowledgment takes on the rules of a sale if the subject matter is property in exchange for property, or of an Ijarah (hire) if the compensation is usufruct. Sulh with silence or denial functions as an oath-ransoming (to avoid the defendant's oath) and dispute resolution for the defendant, while from the plaintiff's perspective it is an exchange (mu'awadah) where he gives up his claim for the compensation. The text then gives a specific sub-ruling: if one settles to waive a claim to a house (i.e., gives up his right), preemption (shuf'a) does not apply; but if one settles to receive a house (acquires it), shuf'a does apply. Another sub-ruling: if after a sulh with acknowledgment, part of the property is proven to belong to a third party (e.g., by evidence of ownership), the defendant (who paid compensation) can recover a proportional part of the compensation from the plaintiff. The passage concludes with four proofs: a Quranic verse from Surah An-Nisa that endorses sulh in marital disputes, a hadith from Hudaybiyyah showing the Prophet made sulh with non-Muslims (proving sulh is permissible in general), a hadith stating sulh is permissible among Muslims except what changes the lawful/unlawful, and a hadith urging people to seek absolution for wrongs before the Hereafter. Finally, an Urdu note summarizes the principle: all sulh is valid unless it involves rendering the haram halal or halal haram.

Key Terms
الصُّلْحُ (al-sulh): A contract or agreement to resolve a dispute by mutual compromise, ending litigation.
إِقْرَارٌ (iqrar): Acknowledgment by the defendant of the plaintiff's claim.
الشُّفْعَةُ (al-shuf'a): Preemption right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property when sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is shuf'a (preemption) obligatory in a sulh involving a house?
A) When a person makes sulh to relinquish his claim to a house (sulh 'an dar).
B) When a person makes sulh to acquire a house (sulh 'ala dar).
C) Shuf'a never applies to sulh.
D) Shuf'a applies only if the sulh is with acknowledgment.

Key Takeaway
Sulh is valid in all three forms, subject to the condition that it does not change the lawful or unlawful.

Agency in Sulh: The Rule of Daman and the Four Cases
Ruling on agency in sulh with four cases and a hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on agency in sulh and determine the validity of a sulh contract based on the agent's guarantee and the four scenarios.

Core Idea
An agent in sulh is not bound unless he guarantees; without guarantee, four scenarios determine validity.

This passage from al-Quduri's matn lays out the core Hanafi ruling on agency (wakala) in sulh contracts. It clarifies when an agent becomes personally liable and when the contract is binding on the principal or the agent. Four distinct cases are presented, each with a different legal outcome based on the agent's actions. A historical hadith is then cited as proof for the validity of sulh through representatives.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the starting principle: an agent appointed to make sulh is NOT personally liable for the payment unless he guarantees (daman) it. The payment is always the principal's responsibility if within authority.
Guarantee is the key trigger for agent liability.
2
Distinguish the four unauthorized scenarios
When the agent acts without the principal's explicit permission, the text lists four possibilities. Read each carefully: (1) pays and guarantees, (2) uses his own property (e.g., 'my thousand' or 'my slave'), (3) says 'a thousand' and hands it over, (4) says 'a thousand' and is silent.
The first three result in a valid and binding sulh; the fourth is suspended.
3
Analyze the role of guarantee and delivery
In cases 1-3, the agent either guarantees payment (daman), uses his own asset, or physically delivers the money. These acts make the sulh immediately binding on the agent. In case 4, silence means no guarantee or delivery, so the contract is suspended.
The common element in valid cases is that the agent undertakes a concrete obligation.
4
Examine the proof (dalil)
The hadith about al-Hasan ibn Ali and Mu'awiya shows that representatives (two men) were sent to negotiate peace. This demonstrates that sulh through agents is valid and that agents can bind their principals in settlement negotiations.
The hadith is cited as a general proof for agency in sulh, not specifically for the guarantee rule.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools may hold that the principal is automatically bound by the agent's sulh even without guarantee. The Hanafi position requires guarantee for agent liability, protecting the principal from unauthorized commitments.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوَمَنْ وَكَّلَ رَجُلًا لِيُصَالِحَ عَنْهُ لَمْ يَلْزَمُ الْوَكِيلَ مَا صَالَحَ عَلَيْهِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَضْمَنَهُ وَالْمَالُ لازم لِلْمُوَكَّل وَإِنْ صَالَحَ عَنْهُ عَلَى شَيْءٍ بِغَيْرِ أَمْرِهِ فَهُوَ عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَوْجُهِ إِنْ صَاحَ بِمَالٍ وَضَمِنَهُ تَمَّ الصُّلْحُ وَلَزِمَهُ الْمَالُ وَكَذَلِكَ إِذَا قَالَ صَالحَتُكَ عَلَى أَلْفِي هَذِهِ أَوْ عَلَى عَبْدِي هَذَا ثُمَّ الصُّلْحُ وَلَزِمَهُ تَسْلِيمُهَا وَكَذَلِكَ لَوْ قَالَ صَاحَتُك عَلَى أَلْفِ وَسَلَّمَهَا وَإِنْ قَالَ صَالحَتُك عَلَى أَلْفِ وَسَكَتَ فَالْعَقْدُ مَوْقُوفٌ فَإِنْ أَجَازَهُ الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ جَازَ وَلَزِمَهُ أَلْفٌ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُجِزْهُ بَطَلَ ال وجه (1) قول التابعى لثبوت وَمَنْ وَكَّلَ رَجُلًا لِيُصَالِحَ عَنْهُ لَمْ يَلْزَمُ الْوَكِيلَ سَمِعْتُ الْحَسَنَ يَقُولُ: «اسْتَقْبَلَ وَاللَّهِ الْحَسَنُ بْنُ عَلِيّ مُعَاوِيَةَ بِكَتَائِبَ أَمْثَالِ الْجِبَالِ، فَقَالَ عَمْرُو بْنُ الْعَاصِ: إِنِّي لأَرَى كَتَائِب لا تُوَلّي حَتَّى تَقْتُلَ أَفْرَانَهَا، فَقَالَ لَهُ مُعَاوِيَةُ: وَكَانَ وَاللَّهِ خَيْرَ الرَّجُلَيْنِ أَيْ عَمْرُو، إِنْ قَتَلَ هَؤُلَاءِ هَؤُلَاءِ، وَهَؤُلَاءِ هَؤُلَاءِ، مَنْ لِي بِأُمُورِ النَّاسِ، مَنْ لِي بِنِسَائِهِمْ، مَنْ لِي بِضَيْعَتِهِمْ، فَبَعَثَ إِلَيْهِ رَجُلَيْنِ مِنْ قُرَيْشٍ مِنْ بَنِي عَبْدِ شَمْسٍ، عَبْدَ الرَّحْمَنِ بْنَ سَمُرَةَ ، وَعَبْدَ اللَّهِ بْنَ عَامِرِ بْنِ كُرَيْرٍ، فَقَالَ : اذْهَبَا إِلَى هَذَا الرَّجُلِ ، فَاعْرِضَا عَلَيْهِ، وَقُولَالَهُ، وَاطْلُبَا إِلَيْهِ. فَأَتَيَاهُ فَدَخَلَا عَلَيْهِ، فَتَكَلَّمَا، (بخاري شريف، بَابُ قَوْلِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم لِلْحَسَنِ بْنِ عَلِيّ رضي الله عنهما ابْنِي هَذَا سَيِّدٌ، غبر 2704) اصول: کوئی صلح کا مال دینے کی ذمہ داری لے لے تو موکل کی اجازت کے بغیر صلح مکمل ہو جائے گی۔
And whoever appoints a man as agent to make sulh on his behalf, the agent is not bound by what he settles for unless he guarantees it, and the payment is obligatory on the principal. If he settles on his behalf for something without his permission, it is of four types: if he settles with a payment and guarantees it, the sulh is complete and the payment is binding on him; similarly if he says 'I have settled with you on this thousand of mine' or 'on this slave of mine', then the sulh is complete and he must deliver it; similarly if he says 'I have settled with you on a thousand' and hands it over; but if he says 'I have settled with you on a thousand' and is silent, the contract is suspended. If the defendant approves it, it is valid and a thousand is binding on him; if he does not approve, it becomes void. Proof: (1) The saying of a Tabi'i: I heard al-Hasan say: 'By Allah, al-Hasan ibn Ali faced Mu'awiya with troops like mountains. Amr ibn al-As said: I see troops that will not turn back until they kill their opponents. Mu'awiya said: And by Allah, he was the better of the two men, O Amr. If these kill those and those kill these, who will handle the affairs of the people, who will take care of their women, who will take care of their farms? So he sent two men from Quraysh... (Bukhari, Book: The Prophet's saying to al-Hasan ibn Ali: This son of mine is a master, no. 2704). Principle: If someone takes responsibility for paying the sulh money, the sulh is completed even without the principal's permission.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage deals with agency (wakala) in sulh contracts. It begins by stating the general rule: when a principal appoints an agent to make sulh, the agent is not personally liable for the payment unless he explicitly guarantees (daman) it. The payment is always the responsibility of the principal. Then four scenarios are given for when an agent acts without explicit authorization. The first three cases (agent pays and guarantees, agent uses his own property, or hands over the amount) result in a valid sulh. The fourth case (silence) results in a suspended contract, depending on the defendant's acceptance. The accompanying hadith about al-Hasan ibn Ali's peace with Mu'awiya is used as a proof that sulh through representatives is valid and that the representatives can negotiate terms. The Urdu usul emphasizes that if the agent takes on the responsibility of paying the settlement amount, the sulh is valid even without the principal's prior permission. This shows the importance of daman (guarantee) in binding the contract.

Key Terms
وَكَالَة (wakala): Agency contract where one person (principal) authorizes another (agent) to act on his behalf in legal matters, including sulh.
ضَمَان (daman): Guarantee or personal liability. If the agent guarantees the payment, he becomes personally bound to pay, and the sulh is valid even without the principal's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following scenarios does an agent's sulh become immediately binding on the agent?
A) When the agent says 'I have settled with you on a thousand' without further action.
B) When the agent says 'I have settled with you on a thousand' and hands over the money.
C) When the agent says 'I have settled with you on a thousand' and remains silent.
D) When the agent says 'I have settled with you on my slave' and hands him over.

Key Takeaway
An agent in sulh is personally liable only when he guarantees the payment; without guarantee, the contract is either suspended or void unless the principal ratifies.

Validity and Completion of Gift (Hibah)
Defines hibah, its pillars, and completion by possession with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three pillars of a valid hibah, explain the role of possession (qabd) in its completion, and identify the correct verbal formulas for a valid offer.

Core Idea
A gift is valid by offer and acceptance, but is completed only by possession; without possession, it remains revocable.

The gift (hibah) is a noble act of generosity, but Islamic law ensures clarity in its execution. In this passage from Al-Quduri, we learn the essential structure of a valid gift: the offer, the acceptance, and the critical role of possession (qabd). Without possession, the gift remains valid but incomplete and revocable. The text also provides the evidence from the Quran and Sunnah, and lists the specific phrases that create a valid offer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pillars of hibah
The text states that hibah is valid by offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul), and completed by possession (qabd). Therefore, the arkan (pillars) of a hibah are ijab, qabul, and qabd. Note that qabd is a condition for completion, but in Hanafi fiqh it is considered a rukn.
Some later scholars differentiate between validity and completion, but Quduri's matn treats qabd as essential.
2
Understand the condition of possession (qabd)
If the donee takes possession in the same session without explicit permission, it is permissible (the session implies permission). If he takes possession after they separate, it is invalid unless the donor specifically permits. This shows that possession must be connected to the contract session or be authorized.
The session (majlis) is the time and place of the contract.
3
Recognize valid offer phrases
The text lists specific words that constitute a valid offer: wahb (gift), nihlah (grant), i'ta' (giving), it'am (feeding with intent), ja'ala (making something for someone), a'martu (life tenancy), and hamaltu (mounting with intent). These must be said with the intention of gift.
Some phrases like 'it'am' require the intention of gift, not just feeding.
4
Examine the evidence
The text provides a Quranic verse (60:8) encouraging kindness, and several hadith: the Prophet accepting gifts (Aishah, Anas, Nu'man, Jabir). These establish the validity of gift contracts and the practice of accepting gifts.
The hadith are used to support the pillars and the requirement of possession.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, determine if a hibah is validly contracted and completed. Consider whether offer and acceptance occurred, whether possession was taken in the session or with permission, and whether the correct phrases were used.
Remember: without qabd, the gift is not binding and can be revoked by the donor.

كِتَابُ الْهِبَةِ قَالَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ: الْهِبَةُ تَصِحُّ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالْقَبُولِ وَتَتِمُّ بِالْقَبْضِ فَإِنْ قَبَضَهُ الْمَوْهُوبُ لَهُ فِي الْمَجْلِسِ بِغَيْرِ أَمْرِ الْوَاهِبِ جَازَ وَإِنْ قَبَضَ بَعْدَ الافْتِرَاقِ لَمْ يَصِحَّ إِلَّا أَنْ يَأْذَنَ لَهُ الْوَاهِبُ فِي الْقَبْضِ وَتَنْعَقِدُ الْهِبَةُ بِقَوْلِهِ وَهَبْتُكَ وَنَحَلْتُكَ وَأَعْطَيْتُكَ وَأَطْعَمْتُكَ هَذَا الطَّعَامَ وَجَعَلْتُ هَذَا الشَّيْءَ لَكَ وَأَعْمَرْتُكَ هَذَا الشَّيْءَ وَحَمَلْتُكَ عَلَى هَذِهِ الدَّابَّةِ إِذَا نَوَى بِالْحُمْلَانِ الْهِبَةَ. وَجْهٌ: (١) الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ الْهِبَةِ تَصِحُّ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالْقَبُولِ قَالَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿لَّا يَنْهَاكُمُ اللَّهُ عَنِ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُقَاتِلُوكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ وَلَمْ يُخْرِجُوكُم مِّن دِيَارِكُمْ أَن تَبَرُّوهُمْ وَتُقْسِطُوا إِلَيْهِمْ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يُحِبُّ الْمُقْسِطِينَ﴾ [سورة الممتحنة 60:8]. وَجْهٌ: (٢) الْحَدِيثُ عَنْ عَائِشَةَ قَالَتْ: كَانَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم يَقْبَلُ الْهَدِيَّةَ وَيُثِيبُ عَلَيْهَا. (صحيح البخاري، باب المكافأة في الهبة، رقم 2585). وَجْهٌ: (٣) الْحَدِيثُ عَنْ أَنَسٍ قَالَ: أَنْفَجْنَا أَرْنَبًا بِمَرِّ الظَّهْرَانِ فَسَعَى الْقَوْمُ فَلَغِبُوا فَأَدْرَكْتُهَا فَأَخَذْتُهَا فَأَتَيْتُ بِهَا أَبَا طَلْحَةَ فَذَبَحَهَا وَبَعَثَ بِهَا إِلَى رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم بِوَرِكِهَا أَوْ فَخِذَيْهَا فَقَبِلَهُ وَأَكَلَ مِنْهُ. (صحيح البخاري، باب قبول هدية الصيد، رقم 2572). وَجْهٌ: (١) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَتَنْعَقِدُ الْهِبَةُ بِقَوْلِهِ وَهَبْتُكَ وَنَحَلْتُكَ عَنِ النُّعْمَانِ بْنِ بَشِيرٍ: أَنَّ أَبَاهُ أَتَى بِهِ إِلَى رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَقَالَ: إِنِّي نَحَلْتُ ابْنِي هَذَا غُلَامًا فَقَالَ: أَكُلَّ وَلَدِكَ نَحَلْتَ مِثْلَهُ قَالَ: لَا قَالَ: فَارْجِعْهُ. (صحيح البخاري، باب الهبة للولد، رقم 2586). وَجْهٌ: (٢) الْحَدِيثُ عَنْ جَابِرِ بْنِ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ: أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «أَيُّمَا رَجُلٍ أَعْمَرَ عُمْرَى لَهُ وَلِعَقِبِهِ فَإِنَّهَا لِلَّذِي أَعْطِيَهَا لَا تَرْجِعُ إِلَى الَّذِي أَعْطَاهَا لِأَنَّهُ أَعْطَى عَطَاءً وَقَعَتْ فِيهِ الْمَوَارِيثُ». (صحيح مسلم، باب العمرى، رقم 1625). أُصُولٌ: هِبَةٌ ایک عَقْد ہے اور عَقْد میں إِيجَاب و قَبُول ضروری ہے۔
Book of Gift. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: The gift is valid by offer and acceptance and is completed by possession. If the donee takes possession in the session without the command of the donor, it is permissible. If he takes possession after separation, it is not valid unless the donor permits him to take possession. And the gift is contracted by his saying: 'I gift you', 'I grant you', 'I give you', 'I feed you this food', 'I make this thing for you', 'I grant you life tenancy (umra) of this thing', 'I make you ride this mount' if by 'carrying' he intends gift. Evidence (1): The verse for establishing that gift is valid by offer and acceptance: Allah says: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who did not fight you in religion and did not expel you from your homes that you be righteous towards them and act justly towards them. Indeed, Allah loves the just.' (Surah al-Mumtahanah, 60:8). Evidence (2): The hadith from 'Aishah: 'The Messenger of Allah used to accept gifts and reward for them.' (Bukhari). Evidence (3): The hadith from Anas: 'We startled a rabbit at Marr al-Zahran, the people tired, I caught it, brought it to Abu Talhah who slaughtered it and sent its hip or thighs to the Messenger of Allah, and he accepted it and ate from it.' (Bukhari). Evidence (1): The hadith for establishing that the gift is contracted by 'I gift you' and 'I grant you' from al-Nu'man ibn Bashir: 'His father brought him to the Prophet and said: I have granted my son this slave. The Prophet said: Have you granted all your children the like? He said: No. So he said: Take it back.' (Bukhari). Evidence (2): The hadith from Jabir ibn 'Abdullah: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Any man who grants a life estate (umra) to another and his descendants, it belongs to the one given to, it does not revert to the giver, because he gave a gift that entered into inheritance.' (Muslim). Principle: A gift is a contract, and in a contract offer and acceptance are necessary.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling from the Book of Gift (Kitab al-Hibah). It begins with the legal definition: a hibah is valid (sahih) through offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul), but it is completed (tatimmu) only by possession (qabd). Possession is a condition for completion, not for validity; without possession, the gift is valid but revocable? Actually, the Hanafi position is that possession is a condition for the gift to be binding and irrevocable. The text then details: if the donee takes possession in the same session without the donor's explicit permission, it is permissible because the session implies permission. But if he takes possession after they separate, it is invalid unless the donor specifically permits. Next, the text lists the verbal formulas that constitute a valid offer for hibah: 'wahb' (gift), 'nihlah' (grant), 'i'ta' (giving), 'it'am' (feeding with intent of gift), 'ja'ala' (making something for someone), 'a'martu' (life tenancy/umra), and 'hamaltu' (mounting on a ride with intent of gift). The hadith evidence includes: (1) Qur'anic verse encouraging kindness and justice, which includes gift-giving; (2) hadith of 'Aishah showing the Prophet accepted gifts; (3) hadith of Anas showing the Prophet accepted a gift of game; (4) hadith of al-Nu'man showing that a grant (nihlah) is a valid type of hibah, but unequal treatment of children is discouraged; (5) hadith of Jabir showing that an 'umra (life grant) is a gift and does not revert to the giver. The Urdu principle reiterates that hibah is a contract requiring offer and acceptance.

Key Terms
هِبَة (hibah): A gift; a contract transferring ownership without compensation.
قَبْض (qabd): Possession; taking physical control of the gifted item.
إِيجَاب (ijab): Offer; the donor's declaration of gifting.
قَبُول (qabul): Acceptance; the donee's verbal acceptance in the session.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, what happens if the donee takes possession of the gifted item after the session has ended without the donor's permission?
A) The gift is valid and complete.
B) The gift is valid but revocable.
C) The gift is not valid (does not take effect).
D) The gift is void ab initio.

Key Takeaway
A hibah is contracted by offer and acceptance but is completed and made binding only by possession; without possession, the gift is revocable.

6 min
Validity and Completion of Hibah in Hanafi Fiqh
States the ruling on hibah's validity and completion with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for a valid hibah and distinguish between possession in the session and after separation.

Core Idea
Hibah is valid by offer and acceptance but completed and binding only by possession.

In the Hanafi school, a gift (hibah) is not a fully binding contract until the donee takes possession. This lesson unpacks al-Quduri's classic formulation: the gift is concluded by offer and acceptance, but its completion and binding effect depend on physical or constructive delivery. We will explore the rules of possession, the required verbal formulas, and the evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah that supports this nuanced structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is hibah? A contract transferring ownership without compensation. The ruling: valid (sahih) by offer and acceptance, completed (tatimmu) by possession.
2
Understand the condition of possession (qabd)
Possession is required for the gift to become complete and binding. Without possession, the donor may revoke the gift.
This is the key Hanafi difference from other schools.
3
Distinguish between possession in the session and after separation
If the donee takes possession in the same session (majlis) without the donor's command, it is valid. After the session, possession is only valid with the donor's explicit permission.
This highlights the contract's dependence on the session's continuity.
4
Identify the verbal formulas that constitute a valid offer
Words like 'wahb'tuka' (I gifted you), 'nahl'tuka' (I bestowed), 'a'taytuka' (I gave), 'at'am'tuka' (I fed), 'ja'altu' (I made for you), and 'hamaltuka' (I mounted you) with intention of gift are valid offers.
Intention matters; e.g., 'hamaltuka' counts only if gift is intended.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i and Maliki schools do not require possession for validity; the contract is binding immediately. The Hanafi school requires possession for completion and binding effect.
This practical difference affects revocability and risk of loss before delivery.

كِتَابُ الْهِبَةِ
قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ - الْهِبَةُ تَصِحُ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالْقَبُولِ وَتَتِمُ بِالْقَبْضِ فَإِنْ قَبَضَهُ الْمَوْهُوبُ لَهُ فِي الْمَجْلِسِ بِغَيْرِ أَمْرِ الْوَاهِبِ جَازَ وَإِنْ قَبَضَ بَعْدَ الافْتِرَاقِ لَمْ يَصِحَّ إِلَّا أَنْ يَأْذَنَ لَهُ الْوَاهِبُ فِي الْقَبْضِ وَتَنْعَقِدُ الْهِبَةُ بِقَوْلِهِ وَهَبْتُكَ وَنَحَلْتُكَ وَأَعْطَيْتُكَ وَأَطْعَمْتُكَ هَذَا الطَّعَامَ وَجَعَلْتُ هَذَا الشَّيْءَ لَكَ وَأَعْمَرْتُكَ هَذَا الشَّيْءَ وَحَمَلْتُكَ عَلَى هَذِهِ الدَّابَّةِ إِذَا نَوَى بِالْحُمْلَانِ الْهِبَةَ
وَجْهُ (1) الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ الْهِبَةُ تَصِحُ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالْقَبُولِ وَتَتِمُ بِالْقَبْضِ لَا يَنْهَاكُمُ اللَّهُ عَنِ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُقَتِلُوكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ وَلَمْ يُخْرِجُوكُمْ مِنْ دِيَارِكُمْ أَنْ تَبَرُّوهُمْ وَتُقْسِطُوا إِلَيْهِمْ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يُحِبُّ الْمُقْسِطِينَ (سورة الممتحنة، 60:8)
وَجْهُ (2) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ الْهِبَةُ تَصِحُ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالْقَبُولِ وَتَتِمُ بِالْقَبْضِ عَنْ عَائِشَةَ قَالَتْ: كَانَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم يَقْبَلُ الْهَدِيَّةَ وَيُثِيبُ عَلَيْهَا. (بخاري، بَابُ الْمُكَافَأَةِ فِي الْهِبَةِ، رقم 2585)
وَجْهُ (3) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ الْهِبَةُ تَصِحُ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالْقَبُولِ وَتَتِمُ بِالْقَبْضِ عَنْ أَنَسٍ قَالَ: أَنْفَجْنَا أَرْنَبًا بِمَرِّ الظَّهْرَانِ فَسَعَى الْقَوْمُ فَلَغَبُوا فَأَدْرَكْتُهَا فَأَخَذْتُهَا فَأَتَيْتُ بِهَا أَبَا طَلْحَةَ فَذَبَحَهَا وَبَعَثَ بِهَا إِلَى رَسُولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم بِوَرِكِهَا أَوْ فَخِذَيْهَا قَالَ فَخِذَيْهَا لَا شَكَّ فِيهِ فَقَبِلَهُ قُلْتُ وَأَكَلَ مِنْهُ قَالَ وَأَكَلَ مِنْهُ ثُمَّ قَالَ بَعْدُ قَبِلَهُ. (بخاري، بَابُ قَبُولِ هَدِيَّةِ الصَّيْدِ، رقم 2572)
وَجْهُ (1) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَتَنْعَقِدُ الْهِبَةُ بِقَوْلِهِ وَهَبْتُكَ وَنَحَلْتُكَ عَنِ النُّعْمَانِ بْنِ بَشِيرٍ أَنَّ أَبَاهُ أَتَى بِهِ إِلَى رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَقَالَ إِنِّي نَحَلْتُ ابْنِي هَذَا غُلَامًا فَقَالَ أَكُلَّ وَلَدِكَ نَحَلْتَ مِثْلَهُ قَالَ لَا قَالَ فَارْجِعْهُ. (بخاري، بَابُ الْهِبَةِ لِلْوَلَدِ، رقم 2586)
وَجْهُ (2) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَتَنْعَقِدُ الْهِبَةُ بِقَوْلِهِ وَهَبْتُكَ وَنَحَلْتُكَ عَنْ جَابِرِ بْنِ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: أَيُّمَا رَجُلٍ أَعْمَرَ عُمْرَى لَهُ وَلِعَقِبِهِ فَإِنَّهَا لِلَّذِي أَعْطَاهَا لَا تَرْجِعُ إِلَى الَّذِي أَعْطَاهَا. (مسلم، بَابُ الْعُمْرَى، رقم 1625)
اصول: ہبہ ایک عقد ہے اور عقد میں ایجاب و قبول ضروری ہے۔
Book of Gift. He said - may Allah have mercy on him - Gift is valid by offer and acceptance and is completed by possession. If the donee takes possession in the session without the command of the donor, it is valid. If he takes possession after separation, it is not valid unless the donor permits him to take possession. The gift is concluded by his saying: 'I gifted you', 'I bestowed upon you', 'I gave you', 'I fed you this food', 'I made this thing for you', 'I gave you this on life-tenancy', and 'I mounted you on this riding animal' if he intends gift by 'mounting'. Proof 1: The verse for establishing that gift is valid by offer and acceptance and completed by possession: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who did not fight you in religion and did not expel you from your homes from being righteous toward them and acting justly toward them. Indeed Allah loves those who act justly.' (Surah al-Mumtahanah, 60:8). Proof 2: The hadith for establishing that gift is valid by offer and acceptance and completed by possession: from Aisha, she said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to accept gifts and reward for them. (Bukhari, Chapter on Recompense for Gift, no. 2585). Proof 3: The hadith for establishing that gift is valid by offer and acceptance and completed by possession: from Anas, he said: We started a hare at Marr al-Zahran, so the people ran after it and became tired, then I caught it and brought it to Abu Talhah who slaughtered it and sent its hind leg or thighs to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). He said: 'Thighs, no doubt about it.' So he accepted it. I said: 'And did he eat from it?' He said: 'And he ate from it.' Then he said afterwards: 'He accepted it.' (Bukhari, Chapter on Accepting Gift of Game, no. 2572). Proof 1 for establishing that the gift is concluded by his saying 'I gifted you' and 'I bestowed upon you': from al-Nu'man ibn Bashir that his father brought him to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and said: 'I have given this son of mine a slave.' He said: 'Have you given each of your children like this?' He said: 'No.' He said: 'Then take it back.' (Bukhari, Chapter on Gift to a Child, no. 2586). Proof 2 for establishing that the gift is concluded by his saying 'I gifted you' and 'I bestowed upon you': from Jabir ibn Abdullah that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Any man who grants a life-tenancy to someone and his descendants, then it belongs to the one to whom it was given; it does not return to the one who gave it.' (Muslim, Chapter on Life-Tenancy, no. 1625). Principle: Gift is a contract, and in a contract, offer and acceptance are necessary.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the definition and validity of hibah (gift) in the Hanafi school. It is from al-Quduri's matn, followed by proofs from Quran, Hadith, and a usul note. The ruling states that hibah is valid (sahih) with offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul), and it is completed (tatimmu) by possession (qabd). Possession in the same session without the donor's explicit command is valid; after separation, it requires permission. This highlights that hibah is a contract that becomes binding only upon delivery. The author then lists the verbal formulas that constitute a valid offer, including words like 'wahb'tuka' (I gifted you) and 'nahaltuka' (I bestowed upon you), and even 'hamaltuka' (I mounted you) if the intention is gift. The proofs include Quran 60:8, which permits kindness to non-hostile disbelievers (implying permissibility of gifts), and several hadith showing the Prophet's acceptance of gifts and his ruling on gifts to children. The usul note reiterates that hibah is a contract requiring offer and acceptance, a fundamental Hanafi principle.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): A contract transferring ownership of property to another without compensation, valid by offer and acceptance, and completed by possession.
قبض (qabd): Physical or constructive possession of the gifted property by the donee, either by taking delivery or by permission of the donor to possess.
ایجاب و قبول (ijab wa qabul): Offer and acceptance; the exchange of verbal statements that create a contractual relationship. In hibah, they are necessary for the contract to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as stated in the text, what happens if the donee takes possession of the gifted property after the donor and donee have left the session (after separation) without the donor's permission?
A) The possession is valid and the gift is completed.
B) The possession is not valid unless the donor permits it.
C) The gift becomes void and must be re-offered.
D) The donee must return the property immediately.

Key Takeaway
Hibah is a contract that becomes binding only upon possession; verbal offer and acceptance alone do not make it irrevocable in the Hanafi school.
